1469317_16_ITEM15_P18_S0	The Partnership speculatively trades commodity futures contracts, and may trade options on futures contracts, forward contracts and other commodity interests.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P19_S0	The objective of the Partnership s business is appreciation of its assets.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P19_S1	It is subject to the regulations of the Commodity Futures Trading Commission (the CFTC ), an agency of the United States ( U.S. ) government that regulates most aspects of the commodity futures industry; rules of the National Futures Association, an industry self-regulatory organization; and the requirements of commodity exchanges and futures commission merchants (brokers) through which the Partnership trades.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P20_S0	The Partnership s financial statements are presented in accordance with U.S. generally accepted accounting principles ( U.S. GAAP ).
1469317_16_ITEM15_P20_S1	Therefore, the Partnership follows the accounting and reporting guidelines for investment companies.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P21_S0	The preparation of the financial statements in conformity with U.S. GAAP requires management to make estimates and assumptions that affect the reported fair value of assets and liabilities, disclosures of contingent assets and liabilities as of December 31, 2015 and 2014, and reported amounts of income and expenses for the years ended December 31, 2015, 2014 and 2013, respectively.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P21_S1	Management believes that the estimates utilized in preparing the Partnership s financial statements are reasonable; however, actual results could differ from these estimates and it is reasonably possible that the differences could be material.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P22_S0	In accordance with the authoritative guidance under U.S. GAAP, fair value is defined as the price that would be received to sell an asset or paid to transfer a liability (i.e. the exit price ) in an orderly transaction between market participants at the measurement date.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P23_S0	In determining fair value, the Partnership uses various valuation approaches.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P24_S0	The authoritative guidance under U.S. GAAP establishes a fair value hierarchy for inputs used in measuring fair value that maximizes the use of observable inputs and minimizes the use of unobservable inputs by requiring that the most observable inputs be used when available.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P25_S0	Observable inputs are those that market participants would use in pricing the asset or liability based on market data obtained from sources independent of the Partnership.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P26_S0	Unobservable inputs reflect the Partnership s assumption about the inputs market participants would use in pricing the asset or liability developed based on the best information available in the circumstances.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P26_S1	The fair value hierarchy is categorized into three levels based on the inputs as follows:
1469317_16_ITEM15_P27_S0	Level 3 Prices, inputs or exotic modeling techniques which are both significant to the fair value measurement and unobservable (supported by little or no market activity).
1469317_16_ITEM15_P28_S0	The availability of valuation techniques and observable inputs can vary among assets and liabilities and is affected by a wide variety of factors, including the type of asset or liability, whether the asset or liability is new and not yet established in the marketplace, and other characteristics particular to the transaction.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P29_S0	To the extent that valuation is based on models or inputs that are less observable or unobservable in the market, the determination of fair value requires more judgment.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P29_S1	Those estimated values do not necessarily represent the amounts that may be ultimately realized due to the occurrence of future circumstances that cannot be reasonably determined.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P29_S2	Because of the inherent uncertainty of valuation, those estimated values may be materially higher or lower than the values that would have been used had a ready market for the asset or liability existed.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P29_S3	Accordingly, the degree of judgment exercised by the Partnership in determining fair value is greatest for assets and liabilities categorized in Level 3.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P30_S0	In certain cases, the inputs used to measure fair value may fall into different levels of the fair value hierarchy.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P30_S1	In such cases, for disclosure purposes, the level in the fair value hierarchy within which the fair value measurement falls in its entirety is determined by the lowest level input that is significant to the fair value measurement.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P31_S0	Fair value is a market-based measure considered from the perspective of a market participant rather than an entity-specific measure.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P31_S1	Therefore, even when market assumptions are not readily available, the Partnership s own assumptions are set to reflect those that market participants would use in pricing the asset or liability at the measurement date.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P32_S0	The Partnership uses prices and inputs that are current as of the measurement date, including prices and inputs during periods of market dislocation.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P32_S1	In periods of market dislocation, the observability of prices and inputs may be reduced for many assets and liabilities.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P32_S2	This condition could cause an asset or liability to be reclassified to a lower level within the fair value hierarchy.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P33_S0	The Partnership values futures contracts at the closing price of the contract s primary exchange.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P33_S1	The Partnership includes futures contracts in Level 1 of the fair value hierarchy, as they are exchange traded derivatives.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P34_S0	The fair value of U.S. government agency bonds and notes is generally based on quoted prices in active markets.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P34_S1	When quoted prices are not available, fair value is determined based on a valuation model that uses inputs which include interest-rate yield curves, cross-currency-basis index spreads, and country credit spreads similar to the bond in terms of issue, maturity and seniority.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P35_S0	U.S. government agency bonds and notes are categorized in Level I or Level 2 of the fair value hierarchy.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P35_S1	As of December 31, 2015 and 2014, none of the Partnership s holdings in U.S. government agency bonds and notes were fair valued using valuation models.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P36_S0	The fair value of U.S. treasury obligations is generally based on quoted prices in active markets.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P36_S1	U.S. treasury obligations are categorized in Level 1 of the fair value hierarchy.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P37_S0	The fair value of corporate notes is determined using recently executed transactions, market price quotations (where observable), notes spreads or credit default swap spreads.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P37_S1	The spread data used are for the same maturity as that of the notes.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P37_S2	If the spread data does not reference the issuer, data that references a comparable issuer is used.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P38_S0	When observable price quotations are not available, fair value is determined based on cash flow models with yield curves, notes, or single-name credit default swap spreads and recovery rates based on collateral values as key inputs.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P38_S1	These valuation methods represent both a market and income approach to fair value measurement.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P39_S0	Corporate notes are categorized in Level 2 of the fair value hierarchy; however, in instances where significant inputs are unobservable, they are categorized in Level 3 of the hierarchy.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P39_S1	As of December 31, 2015 and 2014, none of the Partnership s holdings in corporate notes were fair valued using valuation models.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P40_S0	The inputs or methodology used for valuing securities are not necessarily an indication of the risk associated with investing in those securities.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P41_S0	There were no changes in the Partnership s valuation methodology during the years ended December 31, 2015 and 2014.

1469317_16_ITEM15_P47_S0	Interest income is recorded on an accrual basis.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P48_S0	Gains or losses on futures contracts are realized when contracts are closed.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P48_S1	Net unrealized gains or losses on open contracts (the difference between contract trade price and quoted market price) are reflected in the Statements of Financial Condition.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P48_S2	Any change in net unrealized gain or loss from the preceding period is reported in the Statements of Income (Loss).
1469317_16_ITEM15_P49_S0	Brokerage commissions on futures contracts include other trading fees and are incurred as an expense when contracts are opened, and are recognized as trading gains and losses.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P50_S0	Net realized gains and losses from foreign currency related transactions represent gains and losses from sales of foreign currencies, sales and maturities of futures contracts in foreign markets, currency gains and losses realized between trade and settlement dates on securities transactions, and the difference between the amounts of interest and foreign withholding taxes recorded on the Partnership s books and the U.S. Dollar equivalent of the amounts actually received or paid.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P50_S1	Net unrealized appreciation (depreciation) on foreign currency denominated other assets and liabilities arise from changes in the value of assets, other than investments in securities, and liabilities at fiscal year end, resulting from changes in the exchange rates.

1469317_16_ITEM15_P52_S0	The Partnership engages in futures contracts as part of its investment strategy.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P53_S0	Upon entering into a futures contract, the Partnership is required to deposit with the broker an amount of cash or cash equivalents equal to a certain percentage of the contract amount.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P53_S1	This is known as the initial margin.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P53_S2	Subsequent payments ( variation margin ) are made or received by the Partnership each day, depending on the daily fluctuations in the value of the contract, and are included in unrealized gain (loss) on futures contracts.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P54_S0	Due to broker amounts on the Statements of Financial Condition represent the amount of any short fall in the Partnership s required cash margin.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P55_S0	The Partnership recognizes a realized gain or loss when the contract is closed.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P56_S0	There are several risks in connection with the use of futures contracts as an investment option.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P57_S0	The change in value of futures contracts primarily corresponds with the value of their underlying instruments.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P57_S1	In addition, there is the risk that the Partnership may not be able to enter into a closing transaction because of an illiquid secondary market.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P57_S2	Open positions in futures contracts at December 31, 2015 and 2014 are reflected within the Condensed Schedules of Investments.

1469317_16_ITEM15_P97_S0	The Partnership participates in the speculative trading of commodity futures contracts, substantially all of which are subject to margin requirements.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P97_S1	The minimum amount of margin required for each contract is set from time to time in response to various market factors by the respective exchanges.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P97_S2	Further, the Clearing Broker has the right to require margin in excess of the minimum exchange requirement.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P97_S3	Risk arises from changes in the value of these contracts (market risk) and the potential inability of brokers to perform under the terms of their contracts (credit risk).
1469317_16_ITEM15_P98_S0	All of the contracts currently traded by the Partnership are exchange traded.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P98_S1	The risks associated with exchange-traded contracts are generally perceived to be less than those associated with over-the-counter transactions because, in over-the-counter transactions, the Partnership must rely solely on the credit of its respective individual counterparties.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P98_S2	However, in the future, if the Partnership were to enter into non-exchange traded contracts, it would be subject to the credit risk associated with counterparty non-performance.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P98_S3	The credit risk from counterparty non-performance associated with such instruments is the net unrealized gain, if any.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P99_S0	The Partnership also has credit risk because the sole counterparty to all domestic futures contracts is the exchange clearing corporation.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P99_S1	In addition, the Partnership bears the risk of financial failure by the Clearing Broker.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P99_S2	The Partnership's policy is to continuously monitor its exposure to market and counterparty risk through the use of a variety of financial position and credit exposure reporting and control procedures.
1469317_16_ITEM15_P99_S3	In addition, the Partnership has a policy of reviewing the credit standing of each clearing broker or counterparty with which it conducts business.

( Advisors or the General Partner ), Altegris Portfolio Management, Inc. ( APM ) and the Partnership, Advisors, a Delaware limited liability company and an affiliate of APM, was admitted as a general partner of the Partnership effective immediately prior to the Transaction (defined below).
1469317_17_ITEM1_P1_S1	Pursuant to an internal reorganization of APM, then a general partner of Partnership, and certain affiliated entities, APM merged with and into Advisors on December 31, 2014 (the Transaction ).
1469317_17_ITEM1_P1_S2	By operation of law and pursuant to Paragraph 17 of Partnership s First Amended and Restated Agreement of Limited Partnership, effective as of July 26, 2010, Advisors then assumed the general partner interest of Partnership previously held by APM.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P2_S0	The General Partner has sole responsibility for management and administration of all aspects of the Partnership s business.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P2_S1	Investors purchasing limited partnership interests (the Interests ) in the Partnership ( Limited Partners and together with the General Partner, Partners ) have no rights to participate in the management of the Partnership.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P3_S0	The General Partner is registered with the Commodity Futures Trading Commission ( CFTC ) as a Commodity Pool Operator ( CPO ) and Commodity Trading Advisor ( CTA ), and is a member of National Futures Association ( NFA ).
1469317_17_ITEM1_P3_S1	Quantitative Investment Management, LLC, a Virginia limited liability company formed in May 2003, acts as the Partnership s trading advisor ( QIM or the Advisor ).
1469317_17_ITEM1_P3_S2	QIM became registered as a Commodity Trading Advisor ( CTA ) in 2004 and a CPO in 2005 and is a member of NFA.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P4_S0	Altegris Investments, L.L.C. ( Altegris ), an affiliate of the General Partner, serves as a selling agent of the Interests and acted as the Partnership s introducing broker until January 1, 2011, when Altegris Futures L.L.C. ( Altegris Futures ) replaced Altegris as the Partnership s introducing broker.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P4_S1	On December 31, 2014, Altegris Futures merged with and into its affiliate, Altegris Clearing Solutions, L.L.C. Altegris Clearing Solutions, L.L.C. is registered with the CFTC as an Introducing Broker ( Clearing Solutions or the Introducing Broker ).
1469317_17_ITEM1_P5_S0	The Partnership s term will end upon the first to occur of the following: receipt by the General Partner of an election to dissolve the Partnership at a specified time by Limited Partners owning more than 50% of the Interests then outstanding, notice of which is sent by registered mail to the General Partner not less than ninety (90) days prior to the effective date of such dissolution; withdrawal, admitted or court decreed insolvency or dissolution of the General Partner unless at such time there is at least one remaining General Partner in the Partnership; or any event that makes it unlawful for the existence of the Partnership to be continued or requiring termination of the Partnership.

1469317_17_ITEM1_P7_S0	As of February 28, 2017, the aggregate net asset value of the Interests in the Partnership before redemptions was $31,382,372.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P7_S1	The Partnership operates on a calendar fiscal year and has no subsidiaries.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P8_S0	The Partnership offers three classes of Interests: Class A, Class B and Institutional Interests (each, a Class of Interest ).
1469317_17_ITEM1_P8_S1	The Classes of Interests differ from each other only in the fees that they pay and the applicable investment minimums.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P9_S0	The Partnership s business constitutes only one segment for financial reporting purposes i.e. , a speculative commodity pool.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P9_S1	The Partnership does not engage in sales of goods or services.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P9_S2	Financial information regarding the Partnership s business is set forth in the Partnership s financial statements, included herewith.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P10_S0	The Partnership s objective is to produce long-term capital appreciation through growth, and not current income.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P11_S0	QIM believes that financial markets are not entirely efficient and that numerous small inefficiencies exist and can be exploited through the prudent use of robust analysis and predictive technologies.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P12_S0	QIM currently employs several thousand quantitative trading models that utilize pattern recognition to predict short-term price movements in global futures markets.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P12_S1	All models are tested across large data sets that expose them to a wide range of market, economic, and political environments, as well as a wide range of time frames and interactions.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P12_S2	Only those models that prove to be the most robust, statistically significant, and conceptually diverse are used in actual trading.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P12_S3	The resultant system of models offers what QIM believes to be reliable signals that guide market timing and trade allocation.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P13_S0	QIM s trading strategies and models may be revised from time to time as a result of ongoing research and development seeking to devise new strategies and systems as well as to improve current methods.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P13_S1	The strategies and systems used by QIM in the future may differ significantly from those currently in use due to changes resulting from this research, and Limited Partners will not be informed of these changes as they may occur.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P14_S0	QIM applies risk management procedures that take into account the price, size, volatility, liquidity, and inter-relationships of the contracts traded.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P14_S1	The Partnership s positions are generally balanced in a manner that allocates approximately equal amounts of measured risk to as many distinct markets as possible and during significant drawdowns in equity, QIM will reduce market exposure by scaling back the Partnership s overall leverage.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P15_S0	QIM s trading is generally approximately 95% systematic and 5% discretionary.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P15_S1	All facets of the predictive models, risk management, and trade allocation are fully automated or proceduralized.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P15_S2	In this sense, the trading is systematic.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P15_S3	Discretion of QIM, however, plays a significant role in the pursuit of improvements to the Program.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P16_S0	QIM has different time horizons for the execution of certain trades.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P16_S1	For example, QIM may have certain trades executed at the beginning of the trading day while giving the executing broker limited discretion with respect to other trades.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P17_S0	QIM currently trades or monitors a broad range of tradable markets in currencies, stock indices, interest rates, energy, grains, softs and metals.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P17_S1	QIM may add or delete markets from this universe of tradable markets in its discretion if QIM s research demonstrates that such an addition or deletion would enhance the program s performance.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P18_S0	All markets are futures markets or interbank currency markets.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P19_S0	QIM seeks to profitably trade each of these markets while taking advantage of the diversification available from such a varied universe of futures contracts.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P19_S1	QIM s trading program often takes opposing long and short positions within the same or related classes of correlated futures, which, taken in conjunction with the effect of diversification across a broad range of contracts, generally results in reduced market exposure than trading a single market with similar leverage.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P20_S0	A substantial portion of the equity in the Partnership s account is invested in United States ( U.S. ) government and agency securities and other liquid, high-quality instruments at the direction of the Custodian (as defined below).
1469317_17_ITEM1_P20_S1	QIM will generally maintain an average margin to equity level of between 0% and 20%.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P20_S2	The actual percentage of assets committed to margin at any time may be higher or lower than the target level.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P21_S0	It is expected that between 5% and 20% of the Partnership s assets generally will be held as initial margin or option premiums (in cash or U.S. Treasury Department ( Treasury ) securities) in the Partnership s brokerage accounts at its clearing broker, SG Americas Securities LLC ( SGAS ), a futures commission merchant ( FCM ), and available for trading by QIM in Commodity Interests on behalf of the Partnership.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P21_S1	Interest on Partnership assets held at SGAS in cash or Treasury securities will be credited to the Partnership.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P21_S2	Depending on market factors, the amount of margin or option premiums held at SGAS could change significantly, and all of the Partnership s assets are available for use as margin.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P21_S3	The Partnership may also retain other brokers and/or dealers from time to time to clear or execute a portion of Partnership trades made by QIM.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P22_S0	With respect to Partnership assets not held at SGAS as described above, but deposited with JPMorgan Chase Bank, N.A. (the Custodian ), the portion not held in checking, money market or other bank accounts (and used to pay Partnership operating expenses) will be invested in liquid, high-quality short-term securities at the direction of the Custodian or its affiliate J.P. Morgan Investment Management Inc. ( JPMIM ).
1469317_17_ITEM1_P22_S1	The Partnership s custody and investment management agreement with the Custodian permits the Custodian to invest in U.S. government and agency securities, other securities or instruments guaranteed by the U.S. government or its agencies, CDs, time deposits, banker s acceptances and commercial paper subject in each case to specific diversification, credit quality and maturity limitations.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P22_S2	The Custodian may use sub-advisers to attempt to increase yield enhancement.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P22_S3	The General Partner may direct that a portion of Partnership assets be deposited with other custodians and retain other sub-advisers for the purpose of attempting to increase yield enhancement via other cash management arrangements.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P23_S0	The percentage of the Partnership s assets deposited with these firms is also subject to change in the General Partner s sole discretion.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P23_S1	The Partnership s assets will not be commingled with the assets of any other person.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P23_S2	Depositing the Partnership s assets with banks or SGAS, or other clearing brokers, as segregated funds is not commingling for these purposes.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P24_S0	The Partnership pays all of its ongoing liabilities, expenses and costs.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P24_S1	The General Partner receives a management fee of 0.104% of the month-end net asset value, before deduction for any accrued incentive fees related to the current quarter (the management fee net asset value ), of all Class A Interests (1.25% per annum), 0.104% of the month-end management fee net asset value of all Class B Interests (1.25% per annum) and 0.0625% of the month-end management fee net asset value of all Institutional Interests (0.75% per annum).
1469317_17_ITEM1_P24_S2	QIM receives 30% of quarterly trading profits applicable to each Class of Interest.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P25_S0	Each selling agent selling Class A Interests receives 0.166% of the month-end net asset value of the Partnership apportioned to each Class A Interest sold by such selling agent (2% per annum) and may elect to receive 0.0417% of the month-end net asset value apportioned to any Institutional Interest sold by such selling agent (0.50% per annum).
1469317_17_ITEM1_P26_S0	SGAS paid, during 2016, to the Introducing Broker a portion of the brokerage commissions and transaction fees received from the Partnership as well as a portion of the interest income received on the Partnership s assets.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P26_S1	Monthly brokerage charges equal to the greater of (A) actual commissions of $9.75 per round-turn (higher for certain exchanges or commodities) multiplied by number of round-turn trades, which amount includes other transaction costs; or (B) an amount equal to 0.125% of the management fee net asset value of all Interest holders month-end capital account balances (1.50% annually).
1469317_17_ITEM1_P27_S0	A round-turn is both the purchase, or sale, of a commodity interest contract and the subsequent offsetting sale, or purchase, of the contract.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P27_S1	If actual monthly commissions and transaction costs in (A) above are less than the amount in (B) above, the Partnership will pay the difference to the Introducing Broker as payment for brokerage-related services.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P27_S2	In any month when the amount in (A) is greater than the amount in (B) above, the Partnership pays only the amount described in (A) above.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P28_S0	The Partnership generally pays its operating expenses as they are incurred.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P29_S0	A fixed administrative fee is charged to Class A and Class B Interests and paid to the General Partner equal to 0.0275% of the management fee net asset value of the month-end capital account balance of all such Class A and Class B Interests (0.333% per annum).
1469317_17_ITEM1_P30_S0	The CFTC has delegated to NFA responsibility for the registration of commodity trading advisors, commodity pool operators, futures commission merchants, introducing brokers, swap dealers, major swap participants and, in most cases, their respective associated persons, as well as floor brokers and floor traders.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P31_S0	The Commodity Exchange Act requires commodity pool operators such as the General Partner, commodity trading advisors such as the Advisor and commodity brokers or FCMs such as SGAS and introducing brokers such as the Introducing Broker to be registered and to comply with various reporting and record keeping requirements.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P31_S1	CFTC regulations also require FCMs and certain introducing brokers to maintain a minimum level of net capital.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P31_S2	In addition, the CFTC and certain commodities exchanges have established limits referred to as speculative position limits on the maximum net long or net short speculative positions that any person may hold or control in any particular futures or options contracts traded on U.S. commodities exchanges.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P31_S3	Similar position limits may in the future be put in place with respect to swaps that are exchange-traded or are economically equivalent to exchange-traded swaps or futures contracts.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P32_S0	All accounts owned or managed by the Advisor will be combined for position limit purposes.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P32_S1	The Advisor could be required to liquidate positions held for the Partnership in order to comply with such limits.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P32_S2	Any such liquidation could result in substantial costs to the Partnership.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P32_S3	In addition, many futures exchanges impose limits beyond which the price of a futures contract may not trade during the course of a trading day, and there is a potential for a futures contract to hit its daily price limit for several days in a row, making it impossible for the Advisor to liquidate a position and thereby experiencing a dramatic loss.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P33_S0	Certain deliverable currency forward contracts are subject to limited regulation in the United States, including reporting and recordkeeping requirements.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P34_S0	In addition to the registration requirements described above, the CFTC and certain commodity exchanges have established limits on the maximum net long or net short position which any person may hold or control in particular commodities.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P34_S1	Most exchanges also limit the changes in futures contract prices that may occur during a single trading day.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P34_S2	The CFTC may in the future also implement position limits for certain exempt commodity contracts, including metals and energy contracts, with respect to futures, options on futures, and economically equivalent swaps.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P34_S3	If such position limits are adopted, they could materially affect the Partnership s trading strategy.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P35_S0	Deliverable currency forward contracts are currently subject to only limited regulation in the United States.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P35_S1	The Dodd-Frank Wall Street Reform and Consumer Protection Act (the Reform Act ) was enacted in July 2010, and gave the CFTC jurisdiction over non-deliverable currency forward contracts.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P35_S2	The Reform Act mandates that a substantial portion of over-the-counter derivatives must be executed in regulated markets and submitted for clearing to regulated clearinghouses, and the CFTC may impose such a requirement on non-deliverable currency forward contracts.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P35_S3	The mandates imposed by the Reform Act may result in the Partnership bearing higher upfront and mark-to-market margin, less favorable trade pricing, and the possible imposition of new or increased fees with respect to any swaps entered into by the Partnership.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P36_S0	The Partnership has no operations in foreign countries although it trades on foreign exchanges and other non-U.S. markets.
1469317_17_ITEM1_P36_S1	The Partnership does not engage in sales of goods or services.

1469317_17_ITEM7_P1_S1	Other than any potential market-imposed limitations on liquidity, the Partnership s assets are highly liquid and are expected to remain so.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P1_S2	Market-imposed limitations, when they occur, can be due to limited open interest in certain futures markets or to daily price fluctuation limits, which are inherent in the Partnership s futures trading.

1469317_17_ITEM7_P2_S1	Through December 31, 2016 the Partnership experienced no meaningful periods of illiquidity in any of the markets traded by the Advisor on behalf of the Partnership.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P3_S0	The Partnership raises additional capital only through the sale of Interests and capital is increased through trading profits (if any) and interest income.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P3_S1	The Partnership does not engage in borrowing.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P4_S0	The amount of capital raised for the Partnership should not have a significant impact on its operations, as the Partnership has no significant capital expenditure or working capital requirements other than for capital to pay trading losses, brokerage commissions and expenses.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P4_S1	Within broad ranges of capitalization, the Partnership s trading positions should increase or decrease in approximate proportion to the size of the Partnership.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P5_S0	The Partnership participates in the speculative trading of commodity futures contracts and may trade options on futures contracts and forward contracts, substantially all of which are subject to margin requirements.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P5_S1	The minimum amount of margin required for each contract is set from time to time in response to various market factors by the respective exchanges.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P5_S2	Further, the Partnership s FCMs and brokers may require margin in excess of minimum exchange requirements.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P6_S0	All of the futures contracts currently traded by the Advisor on behalf of the Partnership are exchange-traded.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P6_S1	The risks associated with exchange-traded contracts are generally perceived to be less than those associated with over-the-counter transactions because, in over-the-counter transactions, the Partnership must rely solely on the credit of its trading counterparties, whereas exchange-traded contracts are generally, but not universally, backed by the collective credit of the members of the exchange.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P6_S2	In the future, the Partnership anticipates that it will enter into non-exchange-traded foreign currency contracts and be subject to the credit risk associated with counterparty non-performance.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P7_S0	The Partnership bears the risk of financial failure by SGAS and/or other clearing brokers or counterparties with which the Partnership trades.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P8_S0	The Partnership s success depends primarily upon QIM s ability to recognize and capitalize on market trends in the sectors of the global commodity futures markets in which it trades.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P8_S1	The Partnership seeks to produce long-term capital appreciation through growth, and not current income.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P8_S2	The past performance of the Partnership is not necessarily indicative of future results.

1469317_17_ITEM7_P11_S0	Underperformance was driven by stock indices, while the most profitable sector was currencies, followed by metals.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P12_S0	Early in the month, long stock index and interest rate positions suffered as markets digested hawkish commentary from the Federal Reserve, a rising dollar and continued uncertainty surrounding the U.S. presidential election.

1469317_17_ITEM7_P12_S2	Short positions in interest rate futures acquired mid-month did offset some of those losses as bonds traded to their lowest level in four months.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P12_S3	The U.S. Dollar strengthened throughout the month, benefiting the Partnership s short currency positions.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P12_S4	The bulk of profits in the metals sector were attributed to a short position in gold, for which prices collapsed early in the month.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P12_S5	The Partnership had overall positive returns for the month of November, 2016, with performance driven by stock indices, while metals were the worst performing sector, followed by energies.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P12_S6	The Partnership began the month with significant long U.S. stock index positions.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P12_S7	Initially, markets traded lower ahead of uncertainty about the U.S. presidential election, and post-election, stock markets sold off dramatically only to rebound and close higher by the end of the following trading day.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P12_S8	The Partnership remained long while indices climbed higher through the end of the month.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P12_S9	A long gold position acquired midmonth was the primary driver for losses in the metals sector with that market trading to nine-month lows on continued strength in the equity markets.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P12_S10	In the energies sector, the Partnership sustained losses on short crude oil positions as markets moved higher on speculation that OPEC was nearing an agreement for production cuts.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P12_S11	The Partnership was up for the month of December 2016.

1469317_17_ITEM7_P13_S1	The only sector with negative performance was metals.

1469317_17_ITEM7_P18_S17	Currency losses accrued early in the month and were primarily attributable to short Euro positions as a hawkish European Central Bank meeting sent the currency sharply higher.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P18_S18	The bulk of the losses in energies came in the last week of trading when short crude positions suffered on the news that OPEC had reached a preliminary agreement on the first production cuts in eight years.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P19_S0	The Partnership was flat to slightly negative for the month of April 2016.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P20_S0	Currencies were the most profitable sector, followed by stock indices and metals.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P20_S1	Interest rates were the worst performing sector, followed by energies.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P20_S2	The Partnership came into the month short all stock index contracts, making substantial profits in the first week of trading as equity markets traded lower.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P20_S3	As the month wore on, equity markets recovered on the back of a marginally positive earnings season domestically and the lack of any significant negative headlines internationally.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P20_S4	The Partnership maintained a net short position as markets trended higher, erasing earlier profits and going negative in the sector until equity markets began to falter late in the month on disappointing news from the Bank of Japan as to its leaving rates unchanged and failing to increase stimulus.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P20_S5	This sent equity markets sharply lower and the Yen skyrocketed to new multi-year highs resulting in gains for the Partnership.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P20_S6	While bonds moved lower on equity strength, the possibility of a June rate hike from the FOMC limited the upside as equities sold off.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P20_S7	The Partnership experienced profits in May 2016, with the most profitable sector being stock indices, followed by interest rates and currencies.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P20_S8	Following a late April FOMC statement that lowered the likelihood for a June 2016 rate hike, concerns regarding the global economy were further heightened in the first week of May by weak Caixin PMI data in China and disappointing nonfarm payroll numbers in the United States.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P20_S9	These events caused a flight to quality during early in the month.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P20_S10	During this period, the Partnership traded the Euro-Bund, U.S. 30-Year Bond, Long Gilt, S P and Hang Seng futures contracts to secure gains in the interest rates and stock indices sectors.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P21_S0	As the month progressed, a shift in the Federal Reserve s tone indicated a likely rate hike due to an improving economy, and coupled with substantial short covering and reports of a surge in U.S. new home sales, a strong rally in equity indices and commodities ensued (offset by a decline in global interest rate futures).
1469317_17_ITEM7_P22_S0	The second half of May saw the Partnership benefitting from long positions in U.S. equity indices, while it suffered from short positions in European equity indices and long positions across U.S. Treasuries.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P22_S1	The Partnership was up slightly for the month of June 2016, with the most profitable sector being stock indices, followed by interest rates.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P22_S2	The only negative sector was currencies.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P23_S0	Volatility across global financial markets was largely driven by expectations and reactions around the FOMC decision to hold on an interest rate hike and the U.K. referendum vote.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P24_S0	With the uncertainty of an interest rate hike removed, attention became squarely focused on the outcome of Brexit vote.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P24_S1	As resultant volatility ensued, the Partnership benefitted from positions in the DJ Euro Stoxx 50, Dax, U.S. 30-Year Bond and Gold, and risk exposure leading up to the Brexit vote was reduced.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P25_S0	The Partnership suffered losses during the days preceding the Brexit vote but recouped most of those losses as markets reacted sharply to the surprising decision by the U.K. to leave the European Union.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P25_S1	Over the last three days of June 2016, the Partnership gave back profits in the stock indices sector as global equity markets rallied.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P26_S0	The Partnership experienced net gains for the month of January 2016, with interest rates contributing most of the profits, followed by stock indices; whereas on the negative side, energies were the worst performing sector, followed by currency positions that also suffered declines in the period.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P26_S1	The Partnership maintained short positions in U.S. European stock indices and long positions in interest rates throughout January resulting in strong returns early in the month.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P26_S2	The Partnership initially posted modest gains from short crude oil positions before reversing into substantial long positions thereafter.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P26_S3	After a temporary rally, the price of crude oil resumed its downward trend, causing the long positions to sustain losses by month-end.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P26_S4	The Bank of Japan announced a negative interest rate policy near month-end, with equity markets trading higher while the Yen dropped sharply costing the Partnership a significant portion of the gains it had made earlier in the month.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P26_S5	The Partnership enjoyed solid net positive performance for the month of February 2016, led by gains in stock indices, followed by positive returns in the interest rates, energies and currencies sectors.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P26_S6	The only negative sector for the month was metals.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P27_S0	Short positions in crude oil and stock indices benefitted from global growth early in the period.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P27_S1	Upon reversing the shorts into longs mid-month, the Partnership s positions in stock indices and crude oil profited due to positive economic data and rallying markets, followed by the Partnership reversing again to shorts allowing it to eke out marginal additional gains as equity markets remained choppy through month-end.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P27_S2	A long position in Yen held throughout the month of February was responsible for most of the profits that month in the currencies sector.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P27_S3	The Partnership s losses in the metals sector for the period were attributed to a short Gold position as that market trended higher.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P27_S4	The Partnership sustained a net loss for the month of March 2016, with stock indices being the worst-performing sector, and currencies, interest rates and energies each experiencing slightly negative returns.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P27_S5	The only positive sector was metals for the period.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P27_S6	Losses were sustained in short stock index positions and long interest rate positions were due to strong economic data coupled with dovish statements from the Federal Reserve leading to the perception that, despite improving economic conditions, a rate hike was unlikely in March.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P27_S7	Short positions in currencies and energies added to losses as the U.S. Dollar surged and commodity prices moved higher throughout the period.
1469317_17_ITEM7_P27_S8	The metals sector was the only bright spot in March, as the Partnership began the month short metals, then reversed mid-month into long positions, making back earlier losses and posting moderate gains as Gold rose on continued bullish sentiment.

1469317_17_ITEM7_P40_S0	Futures and options on futures contracts are valued using the primary exchange s closing price.

1469317_17_ITEM7_P40_S2	Corporate notes are generally valued at fair value.

1469317_17_ITEM7_P40_S4	Realized gains and losses from security transactions are determined using the specific identification cost method.

1469317_17_ITEM9A_P0_S0	(a) The General Partner, with the participation of the General Partner s principal executive officer and principal financial officer, has evaluated the effectiveness of the design and operation of its disclosure controls and procedures with respect to the Partnership as of the end of the period covered by this annual report, and, based on their evaluation, has concluded that these disclosure controls and procedures are effective.
1469317_17_ITEM9A_P1_S0	Altegris Advisors, L.L.C., the general partner of the Partnership, is responsible for the management of the Partnership.
1469317_17_ITEM9A_P1_S1	Management of the General Partner ( Management ) is responsible for establishing and maintaining adequate internal control over financial reporting.
1469317_17_ITEM9A_P1_S2	The internal control over financial reporting is designed to provide reasonable assurance regarding the reliability of financial reporting and the preparation of financial statements for external purposes in accordance with generally accepted accounting principles.
1469317_17_ITEM9A_P2_S0	Provide reasonable assurance regarding prevention or timely detection of unauthorized acquisition, use or disposition of the Partnership s assets that could have a material effect on the financial statements.
1469317_17_ITEM9A_P3_S0	Because of its inherent limitations, internal control over financial reporting may not prevent or detect misstatements.
1469317_17_ITEM9A_P3_S1	Also, projections of any evaluation of effectiveness to future periods are subject to the risk that controls may become inadequate because of changes in conditions, or that the degree of compliance with the policies or procedures may deteriorate.
1469317_17_ITEM9A_P4_S0	Management assessed the effectiveness of the Partnership s internal control over financial reporting as of December 31, 2016.
1469317_17_ITEM9A_P4_S1	In making this assessment, Management used the criteria set forth in Internal Control Integrated Framework issued in 2013 by the Committee of Sponsoring Organizations of the Treadway Commission ( COSO ).
1469317_17_ITEM9A_P4_S2	As a result of this assessment and based on the criteria in the COSO framework, management has concluded that, as of December 31, 2016, the Partnership s internal control over financial reporting was effective.
1469317_17_ITEM9A_P5_S0	There were no changes in the Partnership s internal control over financial reporting during the quarter and year ended December 31, 2016 that have materially affected, or are reasonably likely to materially affect, its internal control over financial reporting.

1469317_17_ITEM11_P0_S0	The Partnership has no officers, directors, or employees.
1469317_17_ITEM11_P0_S1	None of the principals, officers, or employees of the General Partner or Altegris receives compensation from the Partnership.
1469317_17_ITEM11_P0_S2	All persons serving in the capacity of officers or executives of the General Partner, the general partner of the Partnership, are compensated by Altegris and/or an affiliate in respect of their respective positions with such entities.
1469317_17_ITEM11_P0_S3	The General Partner receives a monthly management fee equal to 1/12 of 1.25% of the management fee net asset value of the month-end capital account balances attributable to Class A and Class B Interests and equal to 1/12 of 0.75% of the management fee net asset value of the month-end capital account balances attributable to Institutional Interests.
1469317_17_ITEM11_P0_S4	The General Partner also receives a monthly administrative fee equal to 1/12 of 0.333% of the management fee net asset value of the month-end capital account balances attributable to Class A and Class B Interests.
1469317_17_ITEM11_P1_S0	Altegris receives continuing monthly compensation from the Partnership equal to 1/12 of 2% of the month-end net asset value of Class A Interests sold by Altegris.
1469317_17_ITEM11_P2_S0	Clearing Solutions, in its capacity as Introducing Broker to the Partnership, receives compensation for brokerage-related services.
1469317_17_ITEM11_P3_S0	The Partnership will pay monthly brokerage charges equal to the greater of (A) actual commissions of $9.75 per round-turn (higher for certain exchanges or commodities) multiplied by number of round-turn trades, which amount includes other transaction costs; or (B) an amount equal to 0.125% of the management fee net asset value of all Interest holders month-end capital account balances (1.50% annually).
1469317_17_ITEM11_P3_S1	If actual monthly commissions and transaction costs in (A) above are less than the amount in (B) above, the Partnership will pay the difference to the Introducing Broker as payment for brokerage-related services.
1469317_17_ITEM11_P3_S2	In any month when the amount in (A) is greater than the amount in (B) above, the Partnership will pay only the amount described in (A) above.
1469317_17_ITEM11_P4_S0	The Partnership has no other compensation arrangements.
1469317_17_ITEM11_P4_S1	There are no compensation plans or arrangements relating to a change in control of the Partnership.

1469317_17_ITEM15_P15_S0	Altegris QIM Futures Fund, L.P. ( Partnership ) was organized as a Delaware limited partnership in June 2009.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P15_S1	The Partnership's general partner is Altegris Advisors, L.L.C. (the "General Partner").
1469317_17_ITEM15_P16_S0	The Partnership speculatively trades commodity futures contracts, and may trade options on futures contracts, forward currency contracts and other commodity interests.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P17_S0	The objective of the Partnership s business is appreciation of its assets.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P17_S1	It is subject to the regulations of the Commodity Futures Trading Commission (the CFTC ), an agency of the United States ( U.S. ) government that regulates most aspects of the commodity futures industry; rules of the National Futures Association, an industry self-regulatory organization; and the requirements of commodity exchanges and futures commission merchants (brokers) through which the Partnership trades.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P18_S0	The Partnership s financial statements are presented in accordance with U.S. generally accepted accounting principles ( U.S. GAAP ).
1469317_17_ITEM15_P18_S1	Therefore, the Partnership follows the accounting and reporting guidelines for investment companies.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P19_S0	The preparation of the financial statements in conformity with U.S. GAAP requires management to make estimates and assumptions that affect the reported fair value of assets and liabilities, disclosures of contingent assets and liabilities as of December 31, 2016 and 2015, and reported amounts of income and expenses for the years ended December 31, 2016, 2015 and 2014, respectively.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P19_S1	Management believes that the estimates utilized in preparing the Partnership s financial statements are reasonable; however, actual results could differ from these estimates and it is reasonably possible that the differences could be material.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P20_S0	In accordance with the authoritative guidance under U.S. GAAP, fair value is defined as the price that would be received to sell an asset or paid to transfer a liability (i.e. the exit price ) in an orderly transaction between market participants at the measurement date.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P21_S0	In determining fair value, the Partnership uses various valuation approaches.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P22_S0	The authoritative guidance under U.S. GAAP establishes a fair value hierarchy for inputs used in measuring fair value that maximizes the use of observable inputs and minimizes the use of unobservable inputs by requiring that the most observable inputs be used when available.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P23_S0	Observable inputs are those that market participants would use in pricing the asset or liability based on market data obtained from sources independent of the Partnership.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P24_S0	Unobservable inputs reflect the Partnership s assumption about the inputs market participants would use in pricing the asset or liability developed based on the best information available in the circumstances.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P24_S1	The fair value hierarchy is categorized into three levels based on the inputs as follows:
1469317_17_ITEM15_P25_S0	Level 3 Prices, inputs or exotic modeling techniques which are both significant to the fair value measurement and unobservable (supported by little or no market activity).
1469317_17_ITEM15_P26_S0	The availability of valuation techniques and observable inputs can vary among assets and liabilities and is affected by a wide variety of factors, including the type of asset or liability, whether the asset or liability is new and not yet established in the marketplace, and other characteristics particular to the transaction.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P27_S0	To the extent that valuation is based on models or inputs that are less observable or unobservable in the market, the determination of fair value requires more judgment.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P27_S1	Those estimated values do not necessarily represent the amounts that may be ultimately realized due to the occurrence of future circumstances that cannot be reasonably determined.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P27_S2	Because of the inherent uncertainty of valuation, those estimated values may be materially higher or lower than the values that would have been used had a ready market for the asset or liability existed.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P27_S3	Accordingly, the degree of judgment exercised by the Partnership in determining fair value is greatest for assets and liabilities categorized in Level 3.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P28_S0	In certain cases, the inputs used to measure fair value may fall into different levels of the fair value hierarchy.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P28_S1	In such cases, for disclosure purposes, the level in the fair value hierarchy within which the fair value measurement falls in its entirety is determined by the lowest level input that is significant to the fair value measurement.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P29_S0	Fair value is a market-based measure considered from the perspective of a market participant rather than an entity-specific measure.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P29_S1	Therefore, even when market assumptions are not readily available, the Partnership s own assumptions are set to reflect those that market participants would use in pricing the asset or liability at the measurement date.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P30_S0	The Partnership uses prices and inputs that are current as of the measurement date, including prices and inputs during periods of market dislocation.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P30_S1	In periods of market dislocation, the observability of prices and inputs may be reduced for many assets and liabilities.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P30_S2	This condition could cause an asset or liability to be reclassified to a lower level within the fair value hierarchy.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P31_S0	The Partnership values futures contracts at the closing price of the contract s primary exchange.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P31_S1	The Partnership includes futures contracts in Level 1 of the fair value hierarchy, as they are exchange traded derivatives.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P32_S0	The fair value of U.S. government agency bonds and notes is generally based on quoted prices in active markets.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P32_S1	When quoted prices are not available, fair value is determined based on a valuation model that uses inputs that include interest-rate yield curves, cross-currency-basis index spreads, and country credit spreads similar to the bond in terms of issue, maturity and seniority.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P33_S0	U.S. government bonds are categorized in Levels 1 or 2 of the fair value hierarchy.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P33_S1	As of December 31, 2016 and 2015, none of the Partnership s holdings in U.S. government agency bonds and notes were fair valued using valuation models.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P34_S0	The fair value of U.S. treasury obligations is generally based on quoted prices.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P34_S1	U.S. treasury obligations are categorized in Level 2 of the fair value hierarchy.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P35_S0	The fair value of corporate notes is determined using recently executed transactions, market price quotations (where observable), notes spreads or credit default swap spreads.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P35_S1	The spread data used are for the same maturity as that of the notes.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P35_S2	If the spread data does not reference the issuer, data that references a comparable issuer is used.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P36_S0	When observable price quotations are not available, fair value is determined based on cash flow models with yield curves, notes, or single-name credit default swap spreads and recovery rates based on collateral values as key inputs.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P36_S1	These valuation methods represent both a market and income approach to fair value measurement.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P37_S0	Corporate notes are categorized in Level 2 of the fair value hierarchy; however, in instances where significant inputs are unobservable, they are categorized in Level 3 of the hierarchy.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P37_S1	As of December 31, 2016 and 2015, none of the Partnership s holdings in corporate notes were fair valued using valuation models.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P38_S0	The inputs or methodology used for valuing securities are not necessarily an indication of the risk associated with investing in those securities.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P39_S0	There were no changes in the Partnership s valuation methodology during the years ended December 31, 2016 and 2015.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P40_S0	The following table presents information about the Partnership s assets and liabilities measured at fair value as of December 31, 2016 and December 31, 2015:
1469317_17_ITEM15_P41_S0	"Financial Derivative Instruments" for the fair value in each type of contracts within this category.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P42_S0	The Partnership s policy is to recognize any transfers between Level 1 and Level 2 assets as of the Partnership s fiscal year-end.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P43_S0	For the years ended December 31, 2016 and 2015, there were no transfers between Level 1 and Level 2 assets and liabilities.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P43_S1	For the years ended December 31, 2016 and 2015, there were no Level 3 securities.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P44_S0	Security transactions are recorded on the trade date for financial reporting purposes.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P44_S1	Realized gains and losses from security transactions are determined using the specific identification cost method.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P44_S2	Change in net unrealized gain or loss from the preceding period is reported in the Statements of Income (Loss).
1469317_17_ITEM15_P45_S0	Brokerage commissions on securities and other trading fees are reflected as an adjustment to cost or proceeds at the time of the transaction.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P46_S0	Interest income is recorded on an accrual basis.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P47_S0	Gains or losses on futures contracts are realized when contracts are closed.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P47_S1	Net unrealized gains or losses on open contracts (the difference between contract trade price and quoted market price) are reflected in the Statements of Financial Condition.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P47_S2	Any change in net unrealized gain or loss from the preceding period is reported in the Statements of Income (Loss).
1469317_17_ITEM15_P48_S0	Brokerage commissions on futures contracts include other trading fees and are incurred as an expense when contracts are opened, and are recognized as trading gains and losses.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P49_S0	Net realized gains and losses from foreign currency related transactions represent gains and losses from sales of foreign currencies, sales and maturities of futures contracts in foreign markets, currency gains and losses realized between trade and settlement dates on securities transactions, and the difference between the amounts of interest and foreign withholding taxes recorded on the Partnership s books and the U.S. Dollar equivalent of the amounts actually received or paid.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P49_S1	Net unrealized gain (loss) on foreign currency denominated other assets and liabilities arise from changes in the value of assets, other than investments in securities, and liabilities at fiscal year end, resulting from changes in the exchange rates.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P50_S0	J.P. Morgan Chase Bank, N.A. (the Custodian ) is the Partnership s custodian.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P50_S1	SG Americas Securities, LLC (the Clearing Broker ) is the Partnership s commodity broker.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P50_S2	A portion of the Partnership s assets are held as initial margin or option premiums (in cash or Treasury securities) in the Partnership s brokerage accounts at the Clearing Broker.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P50_S3	The Clearing Broker may convert the Partnership s cash in U.S. dollar to foreign currency to facilitate the Partnership s commodity trading activities.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P50_S4	At times, the Partnership may carry foreign cash on loan with the Clearing Broker.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P50_S5	Any net foreign currency on loan will be recognized in Foreign Currency Due to Broker on the Statements of Financial Condition.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P51_S0	The Partnership s Clearing Broker holds margin balances in a single currency, in which all margin requirements can be satisfied in U.S. dollars.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P51_S1	Foreign currency balances can also be used to satisfy margin requirements.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P51_S2	As of December 31, 2016 and December 31, 2015, the Partnership s restricted cash balance on the Statements of Financial Condition of $3,040,054 and $4,225,374, respectively, represents the collateral pledged by the Partnership to satisfy the Clearing Broker s margin requirements in US Dollars.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P51_S3	The Partnership s assets not deposited at the Clearing Broker are deposited with either the Custodian or held in bank cash accounts at Northern Trust Company (and used to pay Partnership operating expenses).
1469317_17_ITEM15_P51_S4	For the Partnership s cash deposited at the Custodian, the Partnership receives cash management services from J.P. Morgan Investment Management Inc. ( JPMIM ).
1469317_17_ITEM15_P52_S0	The Partnership engages in futures contracts as part of its investment strategy.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P53_S0	Upon entering into a futures contract, the Partnership is required to deposit with the broker an amount of cash or cash equivalents equal to a certain percentage of the contract amount.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P53_S1	This is known as the initial margin.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P53_S2	Subsequent payments ( variation margin ) are made or received by the Partnership each day, depending on the daily fluctuations in the value of the contract, and are included in unrealized gain (loss) on futures contracts.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P54_S0	Due to broker amounts on the Statements of Financial Condition represent the amount of any short fall in the Partnership s required cash margin.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P55_S0	The Partnership recognizes a realized gain or loss when the contract is closed.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P56_S0	There are several risks in connection with the use of futures contracts as an investment option.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P57_S0	The change in value of futures contracts primarily corresponds with the value of their underlying instruments.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P57_S1	In addition, there is the risk that the Partnership may not be able to enter into a closing transaction because of an illiquid secondary market.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P57_S2	Open positions in futures contracts at December 31, 2016 and 2015 are reflected within the Condensed Schedules of Investments.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P58_S0	The Partnership s functional currency is the U.S. dollar; however, it transacts business in currencies other than the U.S. dollar.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P58_S1	Assets and liabilities denominated in currencies other than the U.S. dollar are translated into U.S. dollars at the rates in effect at the date of the Statements of Financial Condition.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P58_S2	Income and expense items denominated in currencies other than the U.S. dollar are translated into U.S. dollars at the rates in effect during the period.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P58_S3	Gains and losses resulting from the translation to U.S. dollars are reported in the Statements of Income (Loss).
1469317_17_ITEM15_P59_S0	At times, the Partnership s cash balance could exceed the insured amount under the Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation ( FDIC ).
1469317_17_ITEM15_P59_S1	The Partnership has not experienced any losses in such accounts and believes it is not subject to any significant counterparty risk related to its cash account.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P60_S0	Restricted cash is held as margin collateral deposits for futures transactions.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P61_S0	Offering costs incurred in connection with the ongoing offering of the Partnership s interests are borne by the Partnership.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P61_S1	These costs include, but are not limited to, legal fees pertaining to updating the Partnership s offering documents and materials, accounting and printing costs.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P61_S2	These costs are charged as an expense when incurred.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P62_S0	The Partnership is treated as a partnership for U.S. federal income tax purposes.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P62_S1	As such, the partners are individually liable for their own distributable share of taxable income or loss.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P62_S2	No provision has been made in the accompanying financial statements for U.S., federal, state, or local income taxes.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P63_S0	The Partnership is required to determine whether its tax positions are more likely than not to be sustained upon examination by the applicable taxing authority, including resolution of any related appeals or litigation processes, based on the technical merits of the position.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P63_S1	The tax benefit recognized is measured as the largest amount of benefit that has a greater than fifty percent likelihood of being realized upon ultimate settlement with the relevant taxing authority.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P63_S2	De-recognition of a tax benefit previously recognized results in the Partnership recording a tax liability that reduces ending partners capital.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P63_S3	Based on its analysis, the Partnership has determined that it has not incurred any liability for unrecognized tax benefits as of December 31, 2016 and 2015.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P63_S4	However, the Partnership s conclusions may be subject to review and adjustment at a later date based on factors including, but not limited to, on-going analyses of and changes to tax laws, regulations and interpretations thereof.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P63_S5	The Partnership is subject to income tax examinations by major taxing authorities for all tax years since 2013.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P64_S0	The Partnership recognizes interest and penalties related to unrecognized tax benefits in interest expense and other expenses, respectively.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P64_S1	No interest expense or penalties have been recognized as of and the years ended December 31, 2016, 2015 and 2014.

1469317_17_ITEM15_P83_S0	The Partnership is subject to monthly brokerage charges equal to the greater of: (A) actual commissions and expenses paid to the Clearing Broker by the Partnership; or (B) an amount equal to 0.125% of the management fee net asset value of all Limited Partners month-end capital account balances (1.50% annually) (the Minimum Amount ).
1469317_17_ITEM15_P84_S0	If actual commissions and expenses paid to the Clearing Broker in a month (in (A) above) are less than the Minimum Amount, the Partnership will pay to the Introducing Broker the difference as payment for brokerage-related services, including, but not limited to, monitoring trade, execution, clearing, custodial and distribution services provided to the Partnership.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P84_S1	If actual commissions and expenses paid to the Clearing Broker in a month (in (A) above) are greater than the Minimum Amount, the Partnership pays only the amounts described in (A) above.

1469317_17_ITEM15_P96_S0	The Partnership participates in the speculative trading of commodity futures contracts, substantially all of which are subject to margin requirements.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P96_S1	The minimum amount of margin required for each contract is set from time to time in response to various market factors by the respective exchanges.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P96_S2	Further, the Clearing Broker has the right to require margin in excess of the minimum exchange requirement.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P96_S3	Risk arises from changes in the value of these contracts (market risk) and the potential inability of brokers to perform under the terms of their contracts (credit risk).
1469317_17_ITEM15_P97_S0	All of the contracts currently traded by the Partnership are exchange traded.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P97_S1	The risks associated with exchange-traded contracts are generally perceived to be less than those associated with over-the-counter transactions because, in over-the-counter transactions, the Partnership must rely solely on the credit of its respective individual counterparties.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P97_S2	However, in the future, if the Partnership were to enter into non-exchange traded contracts, it would be subject to the credit risk associated with counterparty non-performance.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P97_S3	The credit risk from counterparty non-performance associated with such instruments is the net unrealized gain, if any.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P98_S0	The Partnership also has credit risk because the sole counterparty to all domestic futures contracts is the exchange clearing corporation.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P98_S1	In addition, the Partnership bears the risk of financial failure by the Clearing Broker.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P98_S2	The Partnership's policy is to continuously monitor its exposure to market and counterparty risk through the use of a variety of financial position and credit exposure reporting and control procedures.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P98_S3	In addition, the Partnership has a policy of reviewing the credit standing of each clearing broker or counterparty with which it conducts business.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P99_S0	The Partnership has a substantial portion of its assets on deposit with the Custodian in U.S. government agency bonds and notes and corporate notes.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P100_S0	Risks arise from investments in bonds and notes due to possible illiquidity and the potential for default by the issuer or counterparty.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P100_S1	Such instruments are also sensitive to changes in interest rates and economic conditions.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P101_S0	In the normal course of business, the Partnership enters into contracts and agreements that contain a variety of representations and warranties and which provide general indemnifications.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P101_S1	The Partnership s maximum exposure under these arrangements is unknown, as this would involve future claims that may be made against the Partnership that have not yet occurred.
1469317_17_ITEM15_P101_S2	The Partnership expects the risk of any future obligation under these indemnifications to be remote.

1469317_18_ITEM7_P48_S0	The General Partner believes that the Partnership s most critical accounting estimates relate to the valuation of the Partnership s assets.
1469317_18_ITEM7_P49_S0	Futures and options on futures contracts are valued using the primary exchange s closing price.
1469317_18_ITEM7_P49_S1	U.S. government agency securities are generally valued based on quoted prices in active markets.
1469317_18_ITEM7_P49_S2	Corporate notes are generally valued at fair value.
1469317_18_ITEM7_P49_S3	Security transactions are recorded on the trade date.
1469317_18_ITEM7_P49_S4	Realized gains and losses from security transactions are determined using the specific identification cost method.
1469317_18_ITEM7_P50_S0	Assets and liabilities denominated in currencies other than the U.S. dollar are translated into U.S. dollars at the rates in effect at the date of the statement of financial condition.
1469317_18_ITEM7_P50_S1	Income and expense items denominated in currencies other than the U.S. dollar are translated into U.S. dollars at the rates in effect during the period.
1469317_18_ITEM7_P50_S2	Gains and losses resulting from the translation to U.S. dollars are reported in income currently.

1469317_18_ITEM9A_P0_S0	(a) The General Partner, with the participation of the General Partner s principal executive officer and principal financial officer, has evaluated the effectiveness of the design and operation of its disclosure controls and procedures with respect to the Partnership as of the end of the period covered by this annual report, and, based on their evaluation, has concluded that these disclosure controls and procedures are effective.
1469317_18_ITEM9A_P1_S0	Altegris Advisors, L.L.C., the general partner of the Partnership, is responsible for the management of the Partnership.
1469317_18_ITEM9A_P1_S1	Management of the General Partner ( Management ) is responsible for establishing and maintaining adequate internal control over financial reporting.
1469317_18_ITEM9A_P1_S2	The internal control over financial reporting is designed to provide reasonable assurance regarding the reliability of financial reporting and the preparation of financial statements for external purposes in accordance with generally accepted accounting principles.
1469317_18_ITEM9A_P2_S0	Provide reasonable assurance regarding prevention or timely detection of unauthorized acquisition, use or disposition of the Partnership s assets that could have a material effect on the financial statements.
1469317_18_ITEM9A_P3_S0	Because of its inherent limitations, internal control over financial reporting may not prevent or detect misstatements.
1469317_18_ITEM9A_P3_S1	Also, projections of any evaluation of effectiveness to future periods are subject to the risk that controls may become inadequate because of changes in conditions, or that the degree of compliance with the policies or procedures may deteriorate.
1469317_18_ITEM9A_P4_S0	Management assessed the effectiveness of the Partnership s internal control over financial reporting as of December 31, 2017.
1469317_18_ITEM9A_P4_S1	In making this assessment, Management used the criteria set forth in Internal Control Integrated Framework issued in 2013 by the Committee of Sponsoring Organizations of the Treadway Commission ( COSO ).
1469317_18_ITEM9A_P4_S2	As a result of this assessment and based on the criteria in the COSO framework, management has concluded that, as of December 31, 2017, the Partnership s internal control over financial reporting was effective.
1469317_18_ITEM9A_P5_S0	There were no changes in the Partnership s internal control over financial reporting during the quarter and year ended December 31, 2017 that have materially affected, or are reasonably likely to materially affect, its internal control over financial reporting.
1469317_18_ITEM10_P0_S0	The Partnership has no officers, directors, or employees.
1469317_18_ITEM10_P0_S1	The Partnership s affairs are managed by the General Partner (although it has delegated trading and investment authority to the Advisor and administrative duties to Altegris).
1469317_18_ITEM10_P0_S2	The General Partner is indirectly owned by AqGen Liberty Holdings LLC ( AqGen ), an entity owned and controlled by (i) private equity funds managed by Aquiline Capital Partners LLC and its affiliates ( Aquiline ), and by Genstar Capital Management, LLC and its affiliates ( Genstar ), and (ii) certain senior management of the General Partner and its affiliates.
1469317_18_ITEM10_P0_S3	Aquiline is a private equity firm located in New York, New York, and Genstar is a private equity firm located in San Francisco, California.
1469317_18_ITEM10_P0_S4	The General Partner s managers and executive officers are Martin Beaulieu and Matthew C. Osborne.
1469317_18_ITEM10_P1_S0	Martin Beaulieu (born 1958) joined Altegris Advisors as its Executive Chairman in July 2016 and is responsible for firm strategy and the day-to-day management of the company.
1469317_18_ITEM10_P1_S1	In March 2016, Mr. Beaulieu joined the Board of Directors of Altegris Advisors parent company.
1469317_18_ITEM10_P1_S2	During the past five years, Mr. Beaulieu was a Managing Director, Co-Head of iShares U.S. ETFs, Head of iShares Wealth Management, and Head of the Leveraged Distribution Group, at BlackRock Investments (August 2012 through October 2015).
1469317_18_ITEM10_P1_S3	Mr. Beaulieu served in several senior management roles for MFS Investment Management, a large mutual fund complex (September 1990 through July 2012).
1469317_18_ITEM10_P1_S4	These roles included acting, at various times, as MFS Vice Chairman, its Head of Global Distribution, its President, and as a National Sales Manager.
1469317_18_ITEM10_P1_S5	During his tenure at MFS, he also served as CEO of MFS/McLean Budden.
1469317_18_ITEM10_P1_S6	He earned a BA degree from Santa Clara University in 1980.
1469317_18_ITEM10_P2_S0	Matthew C. Osborne (born 1964) was appointed Chief Investment Officer of the General Partner in January 2016.

1469317_18_ITEM11_P0_S4	The General Partner also receives a monthly administrative fee equal to 1/12 of 0.333% of the management fee net asset value of the month-end capital account balances attributable to Class A and Class B Interests.
1469317_18_ITEM11_P1_S0	Altegris receives continuing monthly compensation from the Partnership equal to 1/12 of 2% of the month-end net asset value of Class A Interests sold by Altegris.
1469317_18_ITEM11_P2_S0	Clearing Solutions, in its capacity as Introducing Broker to the Partnership, receives compensation for brokerage-related services.
1469317_18_ITEM11_P3_S0	The Partnership will pay monthly brokerage charges equal to the greater of (A) actual commissions of $9.75 per round-turn (higher for certain exchanges or commodities) multiplied by number of round-turn trades, which amount includes other transaction costs; or (B) an amount equal to 0.125% of the management fee net asset value of all Interest holders month-end capital account balances (1.50% annually).
1469317_18_ITEM11_P3_S1	If actual monthly commissions and transaction costs in (A) above are less than the amount in (B) above, the Partnership will pay the difference to the Introducing Broker as payment for brokerage-related services.
1469317_18_ITEM11_P3_S2	In any month when the amount in (A) is greater than the amount in (B) above, the Partnership will pay only the amount described in (A) above.
